Additionally, let <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/09586457-f70c-4630-b3c0-eaef5555d4b8/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0085.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>sim</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span> denote the tendon stiffness matrix calculated from the strain stiffness <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/e6a58aac-07b4-4bbb-bd71-0d1e4ec1708d/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0086.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><mi>k</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>3.40</mn><mo>×</mo><msup><mn>10</mn><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi></mrow></math></span> obtained from viscoelastic measurements used in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2001" data-behaviour="scroll" data-behaviour-ref="#S003-S2001" data-behaviour-callback="focus" data-behaviour-callback-target="#S003-S2001">3.1</a>. The resulting ellipsoids are shown in Figure&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0010" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">10</button>. The ellipsoid obtained using <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/5e90176d-a654-4a81-9fd0-9a8c21876afb/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0087.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>sim</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span> is shown as a blue solid line, and that obtained using <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/485650ca-66f5-4f7a-a600-84b237305ee5/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0088.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>exp</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span> as a green solid line. The measured endpoint compliance values obtained in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2002" data-behaviour="scroll" data-behaviour-ref="#S003-S2002" data-behaviour-callback="focus" data-behaviour-callback-target="#S003-S2002">3.2</a> are plotted as red points. As shown in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0010" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">10</button>, using <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/aa33e62e-8cda-48c2-8bb6-e9cf29791dd4/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0089.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>exp</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span> yields a smaller compliance ellipsoid. The obtained endpoint compliance values in the <i>y</i>-axis and <i>z</i>-axis directions are shown in green in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0004" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">4</button>. As shown in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0004" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">4</button>, while the analytical values using <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/d89c47cf-d4af-4022-b14b-dc89ba9ee915/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0090.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>sim</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span> differ from the measured values by approximately an order of magnitude, the analytical values based on <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/9321a00d-e62d-4b25-a077-d09818615bf9/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0091.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>exp</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span> remain within approximately three times the measured values. Regarding this approximately threefold difference, we believe it is due to factors that do not affect joint control tendon tension values, such as link deflection, minor play in mechanism assembly, and friction in bearings of joint mechanism components connecting links other than pulleys, as well as nonlinearity in tendon stiffness with respect to elongation. From the above, it was found that using equivalent tendon stiffness including the effects of frictional forces acting on the rope in endpoint compliance analysis enables obtaining analytical values closer to measured values.
</p><div class="figureView"><div class="short-legend"><p class="captionText"><span aria-level="3" role="heading"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 10. </span></span> Calculated endpoint compliance ellipse. Blue: The analytical solution using <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/7303bcb1-65fb-4aa0-8114-f27fe2279fc3/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0092.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>sim</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span>. Green: The analytical solution using <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/d470b2db-34ab-45cd-a1af-ad8846301a21/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0093.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">K</mi><mrow><mtext>exp</mtext></mrow></msub></math></span>. Red: Measured values.</p></div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig" data-id="F0010"><img id="d1e2363" src="/cms/asset/885781d3-5a93-4e3d-b6cb-a56f413c66fc/tadr_a_2642632_f0010_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="449" width="500" alt="Figure 10. Calculated endpoint compliance ellipse. Blue: The analytical solution using Ksim. Green: The analytical solution using Kexp. Red: Measured values."></a><div><button class="show-full-size btn btn-sm" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-id="F0010" data-popup-event-type="fig">Display full size</button></div></div><p></p><p>From Table&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="T0001" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="table">1</button>, the joint angle changes in the experiment where the arm tip was pulled in the <i>y</i>-axis positive direction show a maximum error of 0.3 deg compared to the analytical results, indicating good agreement. For the <i>z</i>-axis positive direction, the maximum error between measured values and analytical results was 1.0 deg. This is considered to be due to the influence of gravity, and although the error is larger compared to the <i>y</i>-axis direction, it mostly agrees with the analytical results. From the above, the analytical results are considered to be valid.</p><p class="last">Thus, we have shown through analysis and experiments that the coupled tendon-driven articulated arm Super Dragon can achieve compliance without special control due to the elongation of joint control tendons. Simultaneously, we revealed that analytically calculated endpoint compliance, when the rope becomes long, exhibits errors despite matching the order of magnitude with measured values due to the influence of frictional forces acting on the rope.</p></div></div><div class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1" id="S004"><h2 class="section-heading-2" id="d1e2381">4.  Contact experiments with external environments</h2><p>Utilizing the compliance shown in the previous section, we conducted motion experiments involving contact with obstacles. We conducted two experiments: one where the arm tip contacts an obstacle, and another where part of the arm's gravity is supported by the external environment. Additionally, assuming that investigation equipment is mounted, a 5 kg weight is attached to the tip of Super Dragon.</p><div class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2" id="S004-S2001"><h3 class="section-heading-3" id="d1e2386">4.1.  Contact experiment with horizontally positioned obstacles</h3><p class="last">As shown in Figure&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0011" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">11</button>, we placed an obstacle on the right side as viewed from the base of the arm, and conducted a motion experiment where the arm tip contacts the obstacle and then separates. Here, the installed obstacle is a wooden rectangular parallelepiped, which has a structure sufficiently rigid relative to the force generated by the Super Dragon's endpoint. The data of joint angle measurements and joint torques calculated from tension measurements using Equation&nbsp;(<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref disp-formula"><a data-rid="M0002" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="disp-formula" data-label="equation"><span class="off-screen">Equation</span>2</a></span><span class="hidden" id="M0002"><span class="NLM_disp-formula-image disp-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/a4eb04ce-337c-4629-90e4-0864a6aca8be/tadr_a_2642632_m0002.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"><span class="disp_formula_label_div">(2) </span></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula disp-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt"><mtr><mtd><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">τ</mi><mo>=</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">Rf</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mo>[</mo><mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt"><mtr><mtd><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">R</mi><mrow><mtext>j</mtext></mrow></msub><mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">R</mi><mrow><mtext>wc</mtext></mrow></msub></mtd></mtr></mtable><mo>]</mo></mrow><mrow><mo>[</mo><mtable columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt"><mtr><mtd><msub><mi mathvariant="bold-italic">f</mi><mrow><mtext>j</mtext></mrow></msub></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd><msub><mi>f</mi><mrow><mtext>wc</mtext></mrow></msub></mtd></mtr></mtable><mo>]</mo></mrow></mtd></mtr></mtable></math><span class="mathjaxLabel"><span class="disp_formula_label_div">(2) </span></span></span></span>) during the experiment are shown in Figure&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0012" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">12</button>. Note that in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0012" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">12</button>(a), the data for each joint angle are plotted with solid lines, while the target value for 6th joint is plotted with a dashed line. The arm tip contacted the obstacle at 134 s and separated from the obstacle at 153 s. During this motion, no hardware damage was observed to either the arm or the obstacle. From Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0012" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">12</button>(c), the absolute values of joint torques for the yaw joints (6th, 8th, and 10th joints) increased during contact compared to before contact, due to the arm tip receiving external force in the <i>y</i>-axis direction. It is considered that contact between the arm and the external environment can be detected by the operator measuring these changes in joint torque. In this way, we achieved contact with the external environment without damage by utilizing the compliance of the coupled tendon-driven articulated arm, and demonstrated the possibility of detecting such contact.
</p><div class="figureView"><div class="short-legend"><p class="captionText"><span aria-level="3" role="heading"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 11. </span></span> The arm tip in contact with the wall.</p></div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig" data-id="F0011"><img id="d1e2417" src="/cms/asset/1cffb242-33f6-4c24-af04-7fdebe3bfaaf/tadr_a_2642632_f0011_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="325" width="500" alt="Figure 11. The arm tip in contact with the wall."></a><div><button class="show-full-size btn btn-sm" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-id="F0011" data-popup-event-type="fig">Display full size</button></div></div>
<div class="figureView"><div class="short-legend"><p class="captionText"><span aria-level="3" role="heading"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 12. </span></span> The results of when the arm tip comes into contact with the wall.</p></div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig" data-id="F0012"><img id="d1e2427" src="/cms/asset/d31e79fb-2c7e-4b66-83bb-bf6bb14f337c/tadr_a_2642632_f0012_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="412" width="500" alt="Figure 12. The results of when the arm tip comes into contact with the wall."></a><div><button class="show-full-size btn btn-sm" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-id="F0012" data-popup-event-type="fig">Display full size</button></div></div><p></p></div><div class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2" id="S004-S2002"><h3 class="section-heading-3" id="d1e2430">4.2.  Joint torque reduction through contact with the external environment</h3><p class="last">In this section, we attempted to reduce joint torque through contact between the arm and the external environment. In other words, rather than detecting and avoiding contact with the external environment, we actively exploit such contact. The required joint torque is determined by the arm's posture and tip payload, which in turn determines the target values for driving tendon tension. However, in practice, there are limits to the tendon strength, which constrains the joint torque that can be generated at each joint. Therefore, postures requiring high joint torque are difficult to maintain, and the workspace is limited. Here, the workspace is defined as the set of positions reachable by the end effector in three-dimensional space. Therefore, by utilizing contact with the external environment to reduce the joint torque required for posture maintenance, tendon tension can also be reduced, potentially resulting in expanding workspace. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0013" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">13</button>, we conducted an experiment where the 9th joint (pitch) was brought into contact with an obstacle placed on the ground from above, and then the 10th joint (yaw) was driven to swing the tip left and right. Note that in this experiment, since it is sufficient to obtain a reaction force from the floor, we determined that the obstacle does not need to be rigid and used a cushioned air bed. The joint angle progression and joint torques during this time are shown in Figure&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0014" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">14</button>. The joint torque calculation method is the same as in the previous section. Note that in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0014" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">14</button>(a), the data for each joint angle are plotted with solid lines, while the target value for 9th joint and 10th joint is plotted with a dashed line. The horizontal axis in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0014" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">14</button> shows time, and the 9th joint and obstacle were in contact from 379 to 529 s. As shown in the lower part of Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0014" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">14</button>, the absolute values of joint torques were reduced during this interval. This is because the 9th joint receives an upward reaction force from contacting the obstacle, supporting part of the gravity. Additionally, as shown in Figure <button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0014" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">14</button>, we successfully drove the 10th joint by <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/cf46e672-fd7c-45b5-b8ca-035eee21fd00/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0094.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><mo>±</mo><mn>10</mn><mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace><mrow><mtext>deg</mtext></mrow></math></span> while in contact with the external environment from 403 to 496 s. Thus, we found that joints can be driven while reducing joint torque by utilizing external contact. This makes it possible to significantly expand the arm's workspace.
</p><div class="figureView"><div class="short-legend"><p class="captionText"><span aria-level="3" role="heading"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 13. </span></span> Experiment on reducing joint torque by utilizing the external environment.</p></div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig" data-id="F0013"><img id="d1e2472" src="/cms/asset/f9ccd915-2108-4c6a-8fef-87c6017efcec/tadr_a_2642632_f0013_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="349" width="500" alt="Figure 13. Experiment on reducing joint torque by utilizing the external environment."></a><div><button class="show-full-size btn btn-sm" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-id="F0013" data-popup-event-type="fig">Display full size</button></div></div>
<div class="figureView"><div class="short-legend"><p class="captionText"><span aria-level="3" role="heading"><span class="captionLabel">Figure 14. </span></span> The results of the experiment on reducing joint torque by utilizing the external environment.</p></div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig" data-id="F0014"><img id="d1e2482" src="/cms/asset/e2123c7c-8892-4e85-8d50-115b5915c6db/tadr_a_2642632_f0014_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="500" width="434" alt="Figure 14. The results of the experiment on reducing joint torque by utilizing the external environment."></a><div><button class="show-full-size btn btn-sm" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-id="F0014" data-popup-event-type="fig">Display full size</button></div></div><p></p></div></div><div class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1" id="S005"><h2 class="section-heading-2" id="d1e2485">5.  Conclusion</h2><p>In this paper, we analytically demonstrated that the coupled tendon-driven articulated arm Super Dragon has compliance due to the elasticity of joint control tendons, and confirmed this using actual hardware. Furthermore, utilizing this compliance, we showed that the arm can safely contact the external environment, and demonstrated the possibility of detecting external contact through changes in torque values calculated from tension measurements. Moreover, we experimentally demonstrated that joint torque can be reduced by intentionally contacting the external environment rather than detecting and avoiding such contact. In this experiment, we successfully reduced the required joint torque magnitude of each joint by contacting the external environment and successfully operated the tip joint in that state.</p><p class="last">As future work, we plan to derive a calculation method to identify the contact point position with the arm from changes in torque values during external contact, and conduct principle verification using actual hardware.</p></div><div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="scholixLinksDropZone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div id="coi-statement" class="NLM_sec"><h2 JP22H03668.</div><div class="contrib-notes"><h3 class="section-heading-3">Notes on contributors</h3><div class="addAuthorInfo"><span class="AuthorInfoData"><h4>Koki Hasegawa</h4><div class="NLM_bio" id="B01"><p class="first last"><b>Koki Hasegawa</b> received his B.E. degree from Tokyo Institute of Technology, Japan in 2024, and he is currently a master's student at Institute of Science Tokyo (formerly Tokyo Institute of Technology), Japan. He is a member of the Japan Society of Mechanical Engineers, and the Society of Instrument and Control Engineers.</p></div></span></div><div class="addAuthorInfo"><span class="AuthorInfoData"><h4>Yuki Shizume</h4><div class="NLM_bio" id="B02"><p class="first last"><b>Yuki Shizume</b> received his B.E., and M.E. degree from Tokyo Institute of Technology, Japan in 2022 and 2024, respectively.</p></div></span></div><div class="addAuthorInfo"><span class="AuthorInfoData"><h4>Takahiro Aruga</h4><div class="NLM_bio" id="B03"><p class="first last"><b>Takahiro Aruga</b> received his B.E., and M.E. degree from Tokyo Institute of Technology, Japan in 2019 and 2021, respectively, and received his Doctor of Engineering degree from Institute of Science Tokyo in 2025. He is currently an assistant professor of Institute of Science Tokyo. He is a member of the Japan Society of Mechanical Engineers, the Robotics Society of Japan, and the Japan Society for Design Engineering.</p></div></span></div><div class="addAuthorInfo"><span class="AuthorInfoData"><h4>Gen Endo</h4><div class="NLM_bio" id="B04"><p class="first last"><b>Gen Endo</b> received his B.E., M.E., and Doctor of Engineering degree from Tokyo Institute of Technology, Japan in 1996, 1998, and 2000, respectively. From 2000 to 2007, he worked for Sony Corp. and was also a visiting researcher at the Advanced Telecommunication Research Institute International. In 2008, he joined the faculty of Tokyo Institute of Technology, as an assistant professor. In 2014, he was an associate professor of Institute of Biomaterials and Bioengineering at Tokyo Medical and Dental University. He is currently a professor at Institute of Science Tokyo. He is a member of the Japan Society of Mechanical Engineers, the Robotics Society of Japan, IEEE and the Society of Instrument and Control Engineers.</p></div></span></div></div></div><div class="pb-dropzone no-border-top" data-pb-dropzone="contentNavigationDropZoneFull"><div class="widget gql-content-navigation none  widget-none" id="d59557d1-e1fb-43ae-9542-192f8d2a53a7">

Measurement of the elastic coefficient of joint control tendons </h3><p>This section describes measurements conducted as part of the analysis of Super Dragon's endpoint compliance.</p><p>In a prior study of the analysis of the elastic modulus of synthetic fiber ropes, Takata et&nbsp;al. [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0015" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>15</a></span>] clarified the elongation characteristics of several types of synthetic fiber ropes. Specifically, they confirmed the applicability of the model proposed by Flory et&nbsp;al. [<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0016" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>16</a></span>] to thin synthetic fiber ropes with diameters of approximately 2 mm, such as the joint control tendons of Super Dragon, and then derived viscoelastic parameters based on this model. Flory's model represents the nonlinear elastoplasticity of marine synthetic fiber ropes using a four-element model and a ratchet and spring (Figure&nbsp;<button class="ref show-table-fig-ref" data-id="F0015" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig">A1</button>). The four-element model consists of a spring representing elongation when the loading rate is sufficiently fast, a dashpot representing long-term creep deformation, and a spring-dashpot element representing elongation when the loading rate is sufficiently slow, while the ratchet represents plastic deformation.</p><div class="figureView"><div class="short-legend"><p class="captionText"><span aria-level="3" role="heading"><span class="captionLabel">Figure A1. </span></span> Flory's model&nbsp;[<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref bibr"><a data-rid="CIT0016" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="bibr" data-label="reference"><span class="off-screen">Citation</span>16</a></span>].</p></div><a href="#" class="thumbnail" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-popup-event-type="fig" data-id="F0015"><img id="d1e3003" src="/cms/asset/e8957c45-9ea3-4d16-a484-7642f3729b12/tadr_a_2642632_f0015_oc.jpg" loading="lazy" height="214" width="500" alt="Figure A1. Flory's model&nbsp;[Citation16]."></a><div><button class="show-full-size btn btn-sm" data-behaviour="show-popup" data-id="F0015" data-popup-event-type="fig">Display full size</button></div></div><p>Assuming this model, when tension is instantaneously applied to a synthetic fiber rope specimen at time <i>t</i> = 0 and kept constant, the tension value <span 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt"><mtr><mtd></mtd><mtd><mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt"><mtr><mtd></mtd><mtd><mi>A</mi><mo>=</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd></mtd><mtd><mi>B</mi><mo>=</mo><msqrt><mo>(</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mo>)</mo><mn>2</mn></msup><mo>−</mo><mn>4</mn><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub></msqrt></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd></mtd><mtd><mi>C</mi><mo>=</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub></mtd></mtr><mtr><mtd></mtd><mtd><mi>D</mi><mo>=</mo><mn>2</mn><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub></mtd></mtr></mtable></mtd></mtr></mtable></math><span class="mathjaxLabel"><span class="disp_formula_label_div">(A4) </span></span></span>The measurements were performed using a tensile testing machine (AG-I, Shimadzu Co.), with the rope specimen secured in grips spaced 100 mm apart. The specimen was then tensioned with 1000 N, and the grips repositioned to release the load to a point where the specimen would not slacken (below 5 N), and this state was maintained for 10 min. Finally, the specimen was pulled 1.6 mm and maintained for 10 min while recording tension data.</p><p>For the obtained tension data, we used the simplex method (<span class="monospace">fminsearch</span> function, Matlab) to determine the elastic and viscous coefficients (<span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/fa6c3f22-5aa6-414b-b662-8c018e65b8cb/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0097.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub><mo>,</mo><msub><mi>c</mi><mrow><mn>3</mn></mrow></msub></math></span>) such that the root mean square of the residuals between the measured tension values and <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/51f8a741-b973-46cf-8f19-f48f7f861d0b/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0098.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><msub><mi>f</mi><mrow><mtext>model</mtext></mrow></msub><mo>(</mo><mi>t</mi><mo>)</mo></math></span> given by Equations&nbsp;(<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref disp-formula"><a data-rid="M0011" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="disp-formula" data-label="equation"><span class="off-screen">Equation</span>A3</a></span><span class="hidden" id="M0011"><span class="NLM_disp-formula-image disp-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/13e0fb35-77ea-4cb0-b2d4-3b9e2456df61/tadr_a_2642632_m0011.gif&quot;}"><span class="mml-formula"><span class="disp_formula_label_div">(A3) </span></span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula disp-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;mathjax&quot;}"><math><mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em class="mathjaxLabel"><span class="disp_formula_label_div">(A3) </span></span></span></span>) and&nbsp;(<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref disp-formula"><a data-rid="M0012" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="disp-formula" data-label="equation"><span class="off-screen">Equation</span>A4</a></span><span class="hidden" id="M0012"><span class="NLM_disp-formula-image class="mathjaxLabel"><span class="disp_formula_label_div">(A4) </span></span></span></span>) was minimized. The measurement results are shown in Table&nbsp;A1 – these are the average values from three measurements. In the derivation of endpoint compliance in Section&nbsp;<a href="#S003-S2001" data-behaviour="scroll" data-behaviour-ref="#S003-S2001" data-behaviour-callback="focus" data-behaviour-callback-target="#S003-S2001">3.1</a>, we ignore the viscous coefficients assuming quasi-static motion. Therefore, we modeled the joint control tendons as springs with elastic coefficients <span class="NLM_disp-formula-image inline-formula rs_preserve"><img src="//:0" alt="" class="mml-formula" data-formula-source="{&quot;type&quot;:&quot;image&quot;,&quot;src&quot;:&quot;/cms/asset/d39ee58f-b9ac-4bfd-8e95-a288f3af82c2/tadr_a_2642632_ilm0099.gif&quot;}"></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula and used the elastic coefficient <i>k</i> given by Equation&nbsp;(<span class="ref-lnk lazy-ref disp-formula"><a data-rid="M0013" href="#" data-behaviour="toggle-ref" data-ref-type="disp-formula"
